How GPT-5, Claude, and Gemini are actually trained and served – Reiner Pope

Dwarkesh Patel(1.3M) ·133:41 ·122.8K views · 2026-04-29
Reiner Pope (CEO, MatX) Dwarkesh Patel (Host, Dwarkesh Patel)
Frontier AI progress is increasingly dictated by the physical constraints of "scale-up" domains (single-rack interconnects), where the economic viability of models depends on amortizing memory bandwidth across massive batch sizes to balance the "Memory Wall.
TL;DR Frontier AI progress is increasingly dictated by the physical constraints of "scale-up" domains (single-rack interconnects), where the economic viability of models depends on amortizing memory bandwidth across massive batch sizes to balance the "Memory Wall." The Batch...

TL;DR

Frontier AI progress is increasingly dictated by the physical constraints of "scale-up" domains (single-rack interconnects), where the economic viability of models depends on amortizing memory bandwidth across massive batch sizes to balance the "Memory Wall."

The Economics of Inference: Batch Size and Latency

  • The Batch Size Trade-off: The cost of AI inference is primarily driven by the need to fetch model weights from memory.
      • Amortization: Increasing the batch size (serving more users simultaneously) amortizes the "cost" of fetching weights across more tokens, drastically reducing the cost per token.
      • Latency Lower Bound: For a given hardware setup, there is a hard floor for latency defined by the time it takes to read all model parameters from High Bandwidth Memory (HBM) into the chips. On modern hardware, this "evacuation time" is typically 15–20 milliseconds.
  • The "Balanced" Batch Size: Reiner Pope identifies a "Goldilocks zone" for efficiency where compute time equals memory fetch time.
      • The 300x Rule: For most modern GPUs, the optimal batch size is approximately $300 \times \text{Sparsity Factor}$.
      • DeepSeek Example: For a model like DeepSeek-v3 (which activates a small fraction of its 700B parameters), the optimal batch size is roughly 2,000 unique sequences.
  • Market Tiering: "Fast modes" (e.g., Claude or Cursor) work by running smaller batches to reduce waiting time, but this increases the provider's cost because weights are fetched for fewer tokens. "Slow modes" would offer diminishing returns once the weight fetch is already fully amortized.

Hardware Topology: Scale-Up vs. Scale-Out

  • The Rack as the Unit of Compute: A "rack" (e.g., Nvidia Blackwell NVL72) is the fundamental boundary for high-speed AI work because of the "Scale-up" network.
      • Scale-up Network (NVLink): Connects GPUs within a rack. It is typically 8x faster than the "Scale-out" network.
      • Scale-out Network (InfiniBand/Ethernet): Connects different racks. This is the primary bottleneck for Mixture of Experts (MoE) models, which require "all-to-all" communication.
  • Physical Constraints: The transition from 8-GPU trays (Hopper) to 72-GPU racks (Blackwell) and eventually 500+ GPU domains (Rubin) is driven by the need for larger "Scale-up" domains.
      • Cabling Density: The limit on domain size is often purely physical: the number of cables that can fit in a rack without snapping or blocking cooling, combined with power delivery and weight limits.
  • Inter-Rack Bottlenecks: Moving a model across two racks introduces a communication bottleneck. To avoid this, developers use Pipeline Parallelism, placing different layers of the model on different racks to minimize the data that needs to cross the slower scale-out network.

Model Architecture: Sparsity and the KV Cache

  • The Power of Sparsity: Mixture of Experts (MoE) allows labs to increase "Total Parameters" (model knowledge) without a linear increase in "Active Parameters" (compute cost).
      • Efficiency Gains: Research suggests a sparse model can match the quality of a dense model using only a fraction of the compute, provided the batch size is large enough to handle the memory overhead of the inactive experts.
  • The KV Cache Problem: While pipelining helps manage the memory footprint of model weights, it does not help with the KV Cache (the memory of the ongoing conversation).
      • Memory Footprint: As context windows grow, the KV Cache becomes the dominant memory consumer. Pipelining layers across racks doesn't reduce the total KV cache needed per GPU because the number of sequences "in flight" must increase to keep all racks busy.
  • Model-Hardware Symmetry: Current parallelism strategies (Expert Parallelism within a rack, Pipeline Parallelism between racks) have evolved to physically mirror the internal architecture of the Transformer model.

The Future of Scaling

  • The Memory Wall: Hyperscalers are reportedly spending 50% of their CAPEX on memory. This scarcity is a primary reason why 5T+ parameter models have not yet saturated the market; labs were waiting for the Blackwell generation's increased memory bandwidth and 10-20 terabyte "scale-up" domains.
  • Architectural Divergence: Reiner Pope notes a "cryptographic" similarity in model design: both AI and ciphers use iterative "jumbling" layers. However, while ciphers aim to maximize the "avalanche effect" (where a small input change causes a total output change), AI models use residual connections and layer norms to keep gradients "contained and simple" for optimization.

***

Notable Quotes

Reiner Pope: "If you do not batch together many users, the cost and the economics you get can be like a thousand times worse than if you do batch many users together."
Reiner Pope: "The reason you have this hierarchy of switches rather than one big switch is to manage the cabling congestion—you just need to run a large number of cables... [Rack design] is a competition for space, weight, power, and cooling. Modern racks are pushing all of those to very extreme physical limits."
Dwarkesh Patel: "It’s interesting to me that the best parallelism strategy in practice ends up being one which physically resembles the actual architecture... oh, we have experts, we’re going to put them on different GPUs; oh, we have different layers, we’re going to put them on different racks."
Reiner Pope: "Pipelining totally solves the capacity problem [for weights], but scale-up size helps solve the bandwidth problem. And the bandwidth problem helps you do longer context lengths, which is more and more relevant as these models get more agentic."

And I no longer, in fact, have all-to-all communication between all the GPUs in two racks. And so the rack-to-rack communication ends up being a substantial bottleneck. So the fundamental thing here is that one rack is actually the bound on the size of an expert layer you can do. And so this has been part of what's been driving towards larger and larger interconnect domains. Yeah, before we go on, it's maybe worth you explaining what exactly a rack is. The differences in bandwidth between a rack and within a rack, and the all-to-all versus not all-to-all nature of communication within versus outside. Yeah, and this is a place where it starts to be very different, in fact, between Nvidia, for example, and Google and then others, including us. Generally, a rack is a... it is a physical structure. It's a few meters tall, a meter or two wide, depends on configuration, and it stores some number of GPUs or XPUs, which is typically about 64. The constraints—what constrains it being a certain size—is power delivery, weight, and cooling ability. It ends up being about this size in many cases because of these physical constraints. So then, when I deploy a data center, I've got a data center that may have thousands of these racks. So I've got one of these tall racks, it's got a bunch of GPUs in it and so on, and then I put another rack next to it. You make it sound so easy. Yeah, right, I just drop them in. In Nvidia's case, the communication topology is actually... they put the GPUs on the outside of the rack, and then they put these switches on the inside of the rack. So what this ends up being is that there's a set of switches in here—these are the NVLink switches—and then they run a bunch of cables. Every single GPU has cables going to the switches in the middle. So every GPU goes to the switches in the middle, and then the switches have connections to all the GPUs. So all of the GPUs can talk to all the other GPUs in just like two hops—going to the switch, going to the other GPU. Now, when I want to leave the rack, I end up going via a different path. The GPUs have also a much slower connectivity, which is typically about 8 times slower. So the green that I drew here, in GPU cases, is the NVLink; more generally, it's called the scale-up network. This is the scale-up network. You will typically also have a scale-out network which allows you to connect to, like, some data center switch. So DC switch. And then all of the GPUs will have some connectivity up to some data center switch somewhere, but this is about... this is the scale-out, and it tends to be about 8 times slower in bandwidth. So the challenge, if you want to, for example, layout a mixture of expert layer across two racks, is that half of the GPUs here are going to be wanting to talk to the GPUs here. Half the tokens on these GPUs are going to want to go inside the rack—that's great, they can use the fast scale-up network—but half the tokens are going to want to leave the rack and go to the other rack. And that's not as good; they're going to need to use a much slower network, and so that becomes the bottleneck on the all-to-all pattern. A different choice would be, "Well, why don't I, like, have a big switch here?" and sort of connect everything to some big switching—like, much bigger switch that actually combines the two racks together. There are many ideas in this direction, but in general it becomes... the reason you have this sort of hierarchy of switches rather than one big switch is to manage the cabling congestion. There's this convergent evolution where they both need to jumble information across all their inputs. For cryptographic protocols, it's to make sure that each new input into a hash function will totally scramble what happens. For neural networks, of course, they need to consider how this piece of information changes what you should make of this other piece of information. I thought that's an extremely interesting point. I guess the difference in what they're trying to do—in some sense, they're trying to do the inverse thing—which is cryptographic protocols are trying to take information which has structure and make it look indistinguishable from randomness. One of the biggest attacks against cryptographic ciphers is also to differentiate the cipher. Ciphers run in a different number field; they run in the field of two elements, so just binary, whereas neural nets run in theory in the field of real numbers. So you have to differentiate with respect to binary numbers, but you can absolutely differentiate a cipher and this is called differential cryptanalysis. Basically what it says is that if you take a small difference of the input, it's quite difficult to make the difference to the output be small. The whole job of a well-designed cipher is to make the difference in output very large. So I guess the distinction is that the optimization goals at that point are about complexifying; they don't have the same residual connections or layer norms that we would have. Yeah. LIVE: Fed Chairman Jerome Powell Holds Press Briefing After Warsh Advances Out Of Committee

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·72:26 ·3.9K views · 2026-04-29
Jerome Powell (Chairman, Federal Reserve Board)
> "These legal actions by the administration are unprecedented in our 113-year history...
TL;DR: The Federal Open Market Committee (FOMC) held interest rates steady at 3.5%–3.75% amid elevated inflation and geopolitical uncertainty, while outgoing Chair Jerome Powell confirmed he will remain on the Board as a Governor to safeguard the Fed’s independence against...

TL;DR: The Federal Open Market Committee (FOMC) held interest rates steady at 3.5%–3.75% amid elevated inflation and geopolitical uncertainty, while outgoing Chair Jerome Powell confirmed he will remain on the Board as a Governor to safeguard the Fed’s independence against unprecedented "legal assaults" and political pressure.

Monetary Policy and Rate Decision

  • Interest Rate Hold: The committee maintained the target range for the federal funds rate at 3-1/2 to 3-3/4 percent.
      • Policy Stance: Powell characterized the current stance as "appropriate," sitting at the high end of neutral or "mildly restrictive."
  • Evolving Guidance: There was a "vigorous discussion" regarding the Fed's "easing bias" (the inclination toward cutting rates).
      • Internal Dissent: Three members dissented, favoring a move to a "neutral" stance (where a hike is as likely as a cut) due to stalled progress on inflation.
      • Decision to Wait: Powell opted to maintain current language, arguing there is no "rush" to change guidance given the high degree of uncertainty over the next 60 days.
  • Meeting-by-Meeting Basis: Future adjustments will depend on incoming data; notably, the Fed wants to see the "back side" of the current energy shock before considering rate cuts.

Inflation and External Shocks

  • Current Data: Total PCE inflation rose to 3.5% (12 months ending in March), while Core PCE (excluding food and energy) stands at 3.2%.
  • Geopolitical Pressure: The conflict in the Middle East has pushed Brent crude toward $120 a barrel.
      • Oil Impact: Powell noted that while the Fed typically "looks through" energy shocks, the duration of the current corridor closures remains "unknowable" and may bleed into core inflation (e.g., airfares).
  • Tariff Pass-Through: Powell attributed a portion of core inflation to recent tariffs, which he views as "one-time price increases." The Fed expects this impact to subside within the next two quarters.

Economic Outlook and Labor Market

  • Economic Resilience: The U.S. economy continues to expand at a "solid pace" (2% or better), driven by resilient consumer spending and "insatiable demand" for business investment in data centers.
  • Labor Market Dynamics: The unemployment rate remains low at 4.3%, but other indicators show a softening trend.
      • Hiring Stagnation: Job gains are low, and there is effectively "no new net job creation."
      • Labor Supply: Slower job growth is partially attributed to a decline in labor force growth due to lower immigration and participation rates.
  • Housing Sector: This remains the primary area of economic weakness in contrast to the broader expansion.

Leadership Transition and Fed Independence

  • Powell’s New Role: Following the end of his term as Chair on May 15, Powell will remain on the Board as a Governor for an undetermined period. He intends to keep a "low profile" to avoid being a "shadow chair" to successor Kevin Warsh.
  • Protecting the Institution: Powell explicitly stated his decision to stay is a response to "unprecedented" legal and political attacks from the administration.
      • Legal Finality: He will not leave the Board until the DOJ criminal investigation is "well and truly over" with full transparency and finality.
  • Transition to Warsh: Powell congratulated Kevin Warsh on advancing out of the Senate Banking Committee and expressed confidence in Warsh's ability to maintain Fed independence and build committee consensus.
"These legal actions by the administration are unprecedented in our 113-year history... I worry that these attacks are battering the institution and putting at risk the thing that really matters to the public, which is the ability to conduct monetary policy without taking into consideration political factors." — Jerome Powell
"We think our policy stance is in a good place for us to hold and wait developments... [The Fed is] an institution full of human beings who work super hard to get things right for the benefit of the public. We're all human, don't expect perfection, but do expect us to make decisions without political considerations." — Jerome Powell
"I’ve said that I will not leave the Board until this investigation is well and truly over, with transparency and finality, and I stand by that." — Jerome Powell
Good afternoon. My colleagues and I remain squarely focused on achieving our dual mandate goals of maximum employment and stable prices for the benefit of the American people. The U.S. economy has been expanding at a solid pace. While job gains have remained low, the unemployment rate has been little changed in recent months. Inflation has moved up and is elevated, in part reflecting the recent increase in global energy prices. Today, the FOMC decided to leave our policy rate unchanged. We see the current stance of monetary policy as appropriate to promote progress toward our maximum employment and 2 percent inflation goals. Developments in the Middle East are contributing to a high level of uncertainty about the economic outlook, and we will remain attentive to risks to both sides of our dual mandate. I'll have more to say about monetary policy after briefly reviewing economic developments. Recent indicators suggest that economic activity has been expanding at a solid pace. Consumer spending has been resilient, and business fixed investment has continued to expand at a brisk pace. In contrast, activity in the housing sector has remained weak. In the labor market, the unemployment rate was 4.3 percent in March and has changed little in recent months. Job gains have remained low. A good part of the slowing in the pace of job growth over the past year reflects a decline in the growth of the labor force due to lower immigration and labor force participation, though labor demand has clearly softened as well. Other indicators, including job openings, layoffs, hiring, and nominal wage growth, generally show little change in recent months. Inflation has moved up recently and is elevated relative to our 2 percent longer-run goal. Estimates based on the Consumer Price Index and other data indicate that total PCE prices rose 3.5 percent over the 12 months ending in March. This was boosted by the significant rise in global oil prices that has resulted from the conflict in the Middle East. Excluding the volatile food and energy categories, core PCE prices rose 3.2 percent over the 12 months ending in March. This relatively high rate largely reflects the effects of tariffs on prices in the goods sector. Near-term measures of inflation expectations have risen this year, likely because of the substantial rise in oil prices. Most measures of longer-term expectations remain consistent with our 2 percent inflation goal. Our monetary policy actions are guided by our dual mandate to promote maximum employment and stable prices for the American people. At today's meeting, the committee decided to maintain the target range for the federal funds rate at 3-1/2 to 3-3/4 percent. The economic outlook remains highly uncertain, and the conflict in the Middle East has added to this uncertainty. In the near term, higher energy prices will push up overall inflation. Beyond that, the scope and duration of potential effects on the economy remain unclear, as does the future course of the conflict itself. We will continue to monitor the risks to both sides of our dual mandate. We are well positioned to determine the extent and timing of additional adjustments to our policy rate based on the incoming data, the evolving outlook, and the balance of risks. Monetary policy is not on a preset course, and we will make our decisions on a meeting-by-meeting basis. This is my last press conference as Chair, and I will close with a few thoughts. First, I want to congratulate Kevin Warsh on his advancement out of the Senate Banking Committee this morning. This is an important step forward, and I wish him well as that process continues. The Federal Reserve exists for one fundamental purpose: to foster the economic conditions in which American families and businesses can thrive—stable prices, a strong job market, and a financial system they can depend on. Every decision we make, whether about interest rates or regulatory and supervisory matters or other issues, is made in service of that purpose. Our decisions reflect the collective judgment of the Board of Governors and the Federal Open Market Committee, colleagues who demonstrate analytical rigor, principled judgment, and a genuine commitment to the public interest. Our collaborative and deliberative process has long reflected a shared commitment to finding common ground in service to our mission. This institution is resilient, capable, and staffed by professionals of extraordinary talent and exceptional dedication. It has been a privilege to serve alongside so many great public servants at the Board of Governors and around the Federal Reserve System. The Fed's work is only as effective as the public's understanding of it, and you, the press, are essential to keeping the public informed about what we do and why. The people we serve benefit from your careful reporting. I welcomed the announcement last Friday by the U.S. Attorney for the District of Columbia that she had closed the criminal investigation. She also noted, however, that she would not hesitate to restart the investigation. Over the weekend, the Department of Justice provided assurances that they will not reopen the investigation unless there's a criminal referral from the Fed's Inspector General. And absent such a referral, if they do appeal the recent court decision, they would not seek as part of that appeal to restart the investigation or send new subpoenas. I've said that I will not leave the Board until this investigation is well and truly over, with transparency and finality, and I stand by that. I'm encouraged by recent developments, and I'm watching the remaining steps in this process carefully. My decisions on these matters will continue to be guided entirely by what I believe is in the best interest of the institution and the people we serve. After my term as Chair ends on May 15, I will continue to serve as a Governor for a period of time to be determined. I plan to keep a low profile as a Governor. There's only ever one Chair of the Federal Reserve Board. When Kevin Warsh is confirmed and sworn in, he will be that Chair. Once sworn in as Board Chair, his new colleagues will elect him to chair the FOMC as well. As I regularly point out from this podium, our success in delivering our goals matters for all Americans. I'm confident that the Fed will continue to do its work with objectivity, integrity, and a deep commitment to serve the American people. Thank you, and I look forward to your questions. Thank you, Mr. Chair. Appreciate the kind words about the press, often doesn't come from the podium in different places, but appreciate that. Can you talk about what has gone into your decision to remain on the Board, what kind of criteria are you weighing and how long might you stay? Thank you. Sure. So, you know, my concern is really about the series of legal attacks on the Fed, which threaten our ability to conduct monetary policy without considering political factors. And I want to note here, this has nothing whatever to do with verbal criticism by elected officials. I've never suggested that such verbal criticism is a problem, and neither has anyone else here. But these legal actions by the administration are unprecedented in our 113-year history, and there are ongoing threats of additional such actions. I worry that these attacks are battering the institution and putting at risk the thing that really matters to the public, which is the ability to conduct monetary policy without taking into consideration political factors. It is so important for our economy, for the people that we serve, that they can depend over time on a central bank that operates that way, free of political influence. It's part of the absolute foundation of this amazing economy that we have. It's just one of the many reasons why the U.S. economy is the envy of the world. That piece of institutional architecture separates successful countries from unsuccessful countries. It is extremely important, not for the people who work at the Fed at any given time, but for the people that we serve, that the Fed remain able to conduct monetary policy in a way that doesn't get pulled into politics, trying to help or hurt any particular politician or political party. Stripe Sessions 2026 | Keynote

Stripe(102.0K) ·91:30 ·560.5K views · 2026-04-29
Stripe is repositioning itself as the "economic infrastructure for the AI era," responding to a 2026 inflection point where autonomous agents have become primary economic actors.
TL;DR Stripe is repositioning itself as the "economic infrastructure for the AI era," responding to a 2026 inflection point where autonomous agents have become primary economic actors. Key innovations include real-time "token" billing, a dedicated payments blockchain (Tempo),...

TL;DR

Stripe is repositioning itself as the "economic infrastructure for the AI era," responding to a 2026 inflection point where autonomous agents have become primary economic actors. Key innovations include real-time "token" billing, a dedicated payments blockchain (Tempo), and the ability for agents to autonomously deploy code and execute financial transactions.

The "Agentic" Economy & Autonomous Commerce

Stripe reports a "parabolic rise" in new business creation in 2026, driven by a shift from human-led commerce to agent-led transactions.

  • Agentic Commerce Suite: A new suite allowing businesses to make products "legible" to AI agents. Partnerships with Google (Gemini), Meta, and OpenAI enable customers to buy products directly within AI interfaces.
  • Stripe Projects: A tool for "vibe deployment" that allows AI agents (like Claude Code) to autonomously provision databases, sandboxes, and hosting to launch apps in seconds.
  • Link Wallet for Agents: A secure credentialing system that allows users to set spending "policies" for their AI agents, allowing them to buy and sell software autonomously without exposing raw card data.
  • Machine Payments Protocol (MPP): An open standard for service-to-service payments, enabling APIs to signal to agents that payment is required and how to facilitate it over HTTP.

Financial Infrastructure for the AI Era

To handle the speed of AI agents, Stripe has re-architected its stack to support high-velocity, micro-scale transactions.

  • Metronome & Real-Time Metering: Following the acquisition of Metronome, Stripe now supports real-time token counting and "rating," allowing AI companies to bill for compute/tokens as they are consumed.
  • Tempo Blockchain: Developed with Paradigm, Tempo is a payments-optimized blockchain designed for "streaming payments." It enables sub-cent micropayments via stablecoins to prevent credit risk in high-speed AI environments.
  • Stablecoin Integration: Stablecoins are now a first-class citizen in the Stripe ecosystem, used for global payouts to 160 countries and real-time settlement for AI agents.

The Stripe "Financial Operating System"

Stripe is moving beyond simple payment processing to become a comprehensive, programmable treasury and data hub.

  • Stripe Treasury: A new financial OS offering multi-currency accounts (USD, EUR, GBP, and stablecoins) with 2% cashback on card spend and FDIC insurance in the US.
  • Instant Transfers: Businesses on the Stripe network can now send money to one another instantly and for free, bypassing traditional banking delays.
  • Stripe Database: A Postgres database that provides real-time, structured access to all Stripe data, designed to be queried by both humans and AI agents.
  • Custom Objects & Workflows: A "made-to-order" version of Stripe where businesses can define their own logic (e.g., insurance claims or shipping numbers) natively within the Stripe dashboard.

Next-Generation Fraud & Intelligence

As fraud evolves into "token theft" and trial abuse, Stripe is leveraging its network data to provide "Signals" to developers.

  • Radar Upgrades: New protections specifically targeting AI-native fraud, such as "Multi-Account Abuse" (stealing user credits) and "Free Trial Abuse" (burning inference costs on non-converting users).
  • Stripe Signals: A new API providing direct access to Stripe’s network intelligence. It returns scores and explanations for transaction risk, business trustworthiness, and customer conversion probability.
  • Smart Disputes: An AI-driven tool that compiles evidence for chargebacks, reportedly recovering 18% more revenue and winning cases 3x more often.

So let me walk you through three of the fastest growing fraud patterns we see across the Stripe network. And by the way, also in your tweets. The first is multi-account abuse, and this happens well before any payment credentials are entered. So a bad actor signs up again and again and again, stealing new user credits every time. Across Stripe, one in six sign-ups at AI companies are involved in multi-account abuse. And they've been nearly impossible to stop, until now. Radar evaluates each new account in real time, so you can block before a single token is burned. Our solution is drawing on signals from prior abuse across the entire Stripe network: device fingerprints, and IP addresses, and email domains, and a whole lot more. For one AI company, 80% of the bad actors Radar caught had sailed right through their existing provider. Another fraud pattern that's escalating quickly is free trial abuse. In just the last six months, free trial abuse has more than doubled. And the last month was particularly ugly. For AI businesses, every abusive trial burns real inference costs. One company we work with was bleeding over $500 on tokens to acquire each paying customer. How, you might ask? They were bankrolling 25 trials just to get one conversion. Now, not every failed trial is abusive, but enough of them are that the economics clearly break. And you're left with a painful tradeoff: either you keep the free trial and let abuse eat your margin, or you kill it and grow slow. Well, Radar now removes that tradeoff. You can turn on Free Trial Abuse Prevention in the Stripe dashboard with one click. Radar predicts which trials are abusive, letting good users through while keeping bad actors away from your tokens. Across eight high-growth AI businesses, Radar blocked more than 3.3 million risky free trial attempts in the last month alone. Okay, and then there's pay-as-you-go abuse. So this is a customer coming in consuming thousands of dollars of tokens over the course of the month, getting billed at the end, and then never paying. It's like a Michelin star all-you-can-eat dine-and-dash, but for tokens. Radar now predicts non-payment as that usage accumulates, which means well before the bill comes due, you can require a top-up or cut off service or take whatever action fits your risk tolerance. Inside Wealth: Thiel Capital Managing Director Jack Selby on investment opportunities

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·24:50 ·3.1K views · 2026-04-29
Jack Selby (Managing Director, Thiel Capital) Robert Frank (Wealth Editor, CNBC)
While AI is a revolutionary technology, it is forming a massive, capital-intensive bubble where potential losses could be orders of magnitude larger than the dot-com era due to high infrastructure costs and underpriced Middle Eastern geopolitical risks.
TL;DR While AI is a revolutionary technology, it is forming a massive, capital-intensive bubble where potential losses could be orders of magnitude larger than the dot-com era due to high infrastructure costs and underpriced Middle Eastern geopolitical risks. Scale of...

TL;DR

While AI is a revolutionary technology, it is forming a massive, capital-intensive bubble where potential losses could be orders of magnitude larger than the dot-com era due to high infrastructure costs and underpriced Middle Eastern geopolitical risks.

The AI Bubble and Capital Intensity

  • Scale of Potential Losses: While AI is a transformative technology, Selby warns it is also an "exceptional bubble." Unlike the dot-com era where companies like Webvan failed with relatively small losses, the AI bust will likely involve "two or three more zeros" in terms of incinerated wealth.
  • CapEx Intensity: The primary risk factor is the physical requirement for energy, chips, and data centers.
      • Depreciation Risk: Markets currently calculate a six-year depreciation curve for AI chips; Selby estimates the actual curve is closer to three years due to high-intensity usage.
      • Infrastructure Fragility: If the "math" on data center ROI fails, it could wipe out tens or hundreds of billions in capital expenditures.
  • Survivorship: While publicly traded giants (Google, Amazon) are safe from bankruptcy, Selby anticipates a "browser war" style culling among private AI firms. He predicts OpenAI will survive, though potentially as part of Microsoft.

Geopolitical Risks and the Middle East

  • Underestimated Volatility: Selby argues that global markets have failed to price in the risk of prolonged conflict in the Middle East.
  • Infrastructure Spend: Approximately 25% of all projected AI infrastructure spend over the next five years is sourced from the Middle East.
      • Force Majeure: Ongoing conflict could lead to the cancellation or postponement of massive data center projects.
      • Secondary Market Impact: Highly leveraged companies involved in these projects (citing Oracle as an example of a high debt-ratio firm) could face significant credit and margin pressure if these contracts are stalled.

The "Culling" of Venture Capital

  • Performance Crisis: Selby estimates that two-thirds of venture capital firms currently have "no right to be in business."
      • Lack of DPI: Investors (LPs) are increasingly frustrated by the lack of "Distributed to Paid-In Capital" (actual cash returns).
      • Slow Failure: Selby notes the irony that VC managers advise startups to "fail fast" while they themselves "fail as slowly as humanly possible" to continue collecting fees.
  • Family Office "Tourist" Capital: Frustrated by poor VC returns, many family offices have moved toward direct investing.
      • The "Cocktail Party" Factor: Selby critiques family offices that invest in opaque Late-stage SPVs (Special Purpose Vehicles) for social status rather than conviction.
      • Public vs. Private: He argues that for most investors, buying liquid stocks like Google is a superior way to "scratch the AI itch" compared to high-fee, low-information private SPVs.

Regional Arbitrage and Founder Talent

  • The Coastal Bubble: Currently, over 90% of VC investment is concentrated in California, New York, and Massachusetts—a record high driven by the AI boom.
  • The Arizona Strategy: Through his fund Copper Sky, Selby targets the "other 47 states" to find companies at "far, far less expensive" valuations.
      • Example: He cites Carvana as proof that billion-dollar tech companies can thrive in the Southwest rather than just Palo Alto or Cambridge.
  • The "Talent Scout" Model: Reflecting on Peter Thiel’s success, Selby describes the best VCs as talent scouts (akin to Hollywood agents) who look for "orthogonal talent" and "that twinkle in the eye" rather than specific sectors.

Macroeconomic Outlook: The "K-Shaped" Economy

  • Inflation Persistence: Selby believes inflation is "here to stay" and will widen the existing wealth gap.
  • The Four Seasons vs. Holiday Inn: In an inflationary environment, businesses serving the top of the "K" (wealthy consumers) have inelastic demand and high pricing power, whereas mid-tier businesses (like Holiday Inn) struggle with rising input costs they cannot pass on.
  • AI and Labor: He views AI as a "J-curve"—painful in the short term as it disrupts middle-class jobs, with the potential for long-term recovery that remains uncertain.

***

"The AI bubble when it goes bust will be at least one more zero, probably two, and three more zeros at times of money that literally goes to zero... because when the losers lose, they will wipe out all of that CapEx and investment." — Jack Selby
"Venture capital managers tell their poorly performing companies to fail fast, but they themselves... fail as slowly as humanly possible. There’s a massive disconnect between the two." — Jack Selby
"If you have an insatiable AI itch that you have to scratch, why wouldn't you just buy Google? Why would you sign up for a ride in an opaque SPV with fees on fees on fees with no information rights?" — Jack Selby
He was one of the founders of PayPal. He runs Copper Sky, the Arizona VC fund, and he helps run Thiel Capital, the family office of tech billionaire Peter Thiel. Jack Selby is a triple threat of investing. I sat down with him at Riverwood Capital's LatAm Tech Forum in Miami. Jack, thanks so much for joining us today. So, you said recently that AI had the potential of being the largest bubble of private tech investing ever. We could see hundreds of billions of dollars of tech wealth or investment wealth incinerated. Do you still think that, and why? Thanks for having me, Robert. I think AI is probably the most revolutionary technology that we have seen in our lifetime, so I just want to disclaim that up front. And that's a lot, because you were an early investor in Facebook and you were one of the guys who helped start PayPal. You've been on the forefront of a lot of internet revolutions. You think this is bigger than any of that? That's right. I'm not a Luddite. AI is a real thing. It's very positive, but it can also be two things at the same time. It can be a revolutionary technology and also it can be an exceptional bubble. And I think that's what's going on. So, there'll be extreme winners in the AI movement that's going on now and there'll also be some real losers. Those losers will be orders of magnitude larger than any of the losers that we've seen before. For example, you were referencing our dot-com era experience with PayPal. There were Webvan, there were other companies that went bust. The AI bubble when it goes bust will be at least one more zero, probably two and three more zeros, at times of money that literally goes to zero. Why is that? Why do you think that many of these companies won't make it? It's a function of the fact that AI is so CapEx intensive. So, if you're building Pets.com, you don't need a lot of CapEx to build that business. Whereas today, you need energy, you need data centers, and you need chips. It's much more capital intensive, so when the winners win, they'll win, but when the losers lose, they will wipe out all of that CapEx and investment. That's tens, if not hundreds of billions of dollars. But unlike the dot-com era, we do have a lot of AI startups, but most of the capital is going toward the big five, six, or seven, whatever you want to call them. Are you saying that some of those companies—we're talking about Anthropic, we're talking about OpenAI, ChatGPT, we're talking about Amazon, we're talking about Alphabet, we're talking about these huge compute companies—I mean, you're not saying any of those are going to fail, right? None of the publicly traded companies are obviously going to go bust. Google's not going bust anytime soon. But in terms of some of the private companies, the best analogy I can give you, and it's far from perfect, is to think back to the dot-com era. Not to date myself again, but think about all the different browser companies that started up back then. So, you had Infoseek and you had Ask Jeeves and you had all these different companies and how many of those actually survived? There was a little company called Google that came along and leapfrogged a company that had been kind of the mainstay and the incumbent, which was Yahoo. So, Yahoo still exists today, but Google is what it is today and all those others are gone. So what if that is analogous? What are the equivalents of those companies that might be gone today? Well, do you think there's any risk that OpenAI doesn't make it, doesn't become one of the big survivors and winners of this game? I think OpenAI will absolutely survive. Now, whether they get tucked back into Microsoft at some point down the road, that's an open question. And what about on the chip space? We've seen Nvidia, we've seen a lot of the chip companies, the sort of "picks and shovel" thesis about AI. Do you think that's valid? Do you think that will continue for a while, or do you think that phase of the revolution is going to end at some point, and when? So it's another answer where there's a bit of a yes or no. I think the railroad analogy of how we're laying tracks for the future and it's value-add no matter what—I don't think that analogy works very well. You're building these huge data centers all over the place and if there is a bump in the road, there is a problem. A lot of these data centers are filled with chips that are running 24/7, 365 as if they're in sixth gear if they're an automobile. They're running so quickly that one of the questions that's come up a lot in the financial press is: what is the depreciation curve on these chips? Previously, they'd estimated that it was six years on average. It's probably somewhat closer to half of that. And so if these chips are depreciating twice as fast as what people are otherwise calculating, then what do you do? So you have to buy new chips or your chips are half as valuable, and that means that some of these data centers may not survive, or at least the math may not be as good as what it was originally thought. So you think the depreciation could be more like three years instead of six? I think the depreciation curve is far, far, far more... it's a faster depreciation curve than what's being depicted. Right. Let's talk about SpaceX, because you were an early investor in SpaceX. I believe you sold out of most or all your position in 2022. They're about to come public with a valuation they're talking about of certainly more than one and a quarter trillion dollars. Do you regret selling? What do you think of that as a company right now? Well, listen, I was extremely lucky and fortunate to get into SpaceX early. So I have absolutely nothing to complain about. You did well, even selling in 2022, you did well. I have nothing to complain about. Peter got a lot of flak when he sold out of Facebook back in, I think, 2010, but he was literally Mark Zuckerberg's first check. I wasn't Elon's first check into SpaceX, but everything was fine. So don't cry for me, certainly. SpaceX is truly a once-in-a-lifetime type company. We may not see another one like it for the rest of our lives. It's exceptional. So I'm just really excited to see what happens when it goes public. Do you think, would you invest in it now, and why not? Do you think the valuation is just... you're not going to get that early gain that you got and so therefore you're moving on to the next company? Or do you think it's still a good investment for retail investors? So, my comment would be that I don't have anything specific to say about SpaceX because I just don't look at late-stage private companies as an investor. That's not what I do. So, it's no offense to SpaceX; it very well could be an amazing investment, but it's just not what I do for a living. Great. And you do a lot of things for a living right now. You're a managing director of Thiel Capital, which is Peter Thiel's family office. You also are the GP of Copper Sky and you're raising a new fund there. Let's talk about Peter Thiel. I know that that's not your primary responsibility; there are a lot of other people that manage that fund for him and that work with him. But broadly speaking, if we look at what he's been investing in with his own private capital, it seems like he likes biotech, he likes drone companies—particularly those in Europe—he's investing a lot in those, and he seems to like, just broadly as does everybody, AI. So, as a guy who knows Peter, what do you think he'd like to do or see as exciting right now as personal investments? Sure. So, I'm not a spokesman for Peter, so I have to disclaim that up front. And I've had the good fortune of working with Peter now for 28 years. So, I know him pretty well. I think one thing that Peter is really, really good at, and it's the reason why Founders Fund is named Founders Fund, is that he is really good at identifying rockstar young entrepreneurs. And he's sector-agnostic about that. So, whether it's drones or AI or any of the different sectors you just talked about, I think there's a degree to which Peter thinks about things from an agnostic perspective and he's just looking for true orthogonal talent. How, what does he look for? What do you see as the common qualities that he tends to like when he finds a founder? He's like, "I don't care what this guy does, this is a founder I want to invest in." Well, again, not speaking for Peter, but I think one of the aspects of venture capital is that you can skin the cat many different ways. I think one of the ways though that Peter's been very good at with the Founders Fund team in particular is—and the reason why the firm is named such is—that the job is almost like you're a talent scout. You work for CAA and you sit across from aspiring, amazing young entrepreneurs all day long and you put in your 10,000 hours. If you do that, you develop almost like an innate sense of who actually has that twinkle in their eye to become the next Elon and who doesn't. And if you do enough of those reps, you get that sense. I think Peter has as good of a sense arguably as anyone in the world. So you think it's not looking at, let's say, "this guy has started and failed three times, I like to find founders who've failed three times," or "this guy has done X." You think it's more instinctual when he meets somebody, that you just know when you meet them? That's right. I was doing an event a couple weeks ago and a fellow panelist made a comment that I strongly disagreed with. His comment was basically that AI is going to replace the job of the venture capitalist. And so my pushback isn't trying to be defensive—everyone's entitled to their own opinion—but I think, when is AI going to paint a Picasso? When is AI going to write an Oscar-worthy script? I would argue that's a long time in the future. So similarly, I think what venture capital does, just like any other business, is that you sit across from other human beings and you make an assessment: do you want to do business with that person? Venture capital, I know it has a sexy reputation, but it's no different than any other business in that regard. I want to ask you what you're investing in now personally. You've got a second fund at Copper Sky; the first one was I think about $100 million, the second one is $300 million. What's the focus of that second fund? The first one was quite local in terms of you were going to be in Arizona investing locally. What's the second fund and where do you see the biggest opportunities right now for that fund? So, the first fund we focused on the Southwest area of the United States, Arizona in particular, where I've lived since our PayPal days. And so the idea is, one of the biggest phenomena that's going on in venture capital today is that investment last year—the numbers aren't in yet—but probably 90% plus of all venture capital investment went to California, New York, and Massachusetts. That is an all-time high. The previous high was in 2021 and it was just above 60%. So it's a record, record high and it's highly, highly correlated to the AI phenomenon. So the good news, glass half full, is if you get outside of those three states and go to the other 47 states, the deals, the investment opportunities are far, far, far less expensive. And that's what we do. We're basically an arbitrage non-coastal, finding great deals that are at reasonable prices, coach them up, and then if they want to go to the coast, we can help them do that given our background. So, it's not that you just dislike Democratic blue states, because those are all Democratic blue states. I love Democratic blue states; I hate expensive deals. But the best companies in AI might just be in California, New York, and Massachusetts. Are you missing out because geographically you're drawing what in a digital age is sort of an arbitrary line? Sure. So, is the next OpenAI or Anthropic going to come out of Phoenix? Probably not. But can you tell me what the largest tech company is that's come out of Arizona? I won't put you on the spot; it's Carvana. Carvana is a perfectly reasonable technology company worth billions and billions of dollars that has come out of Arizona. And it could come out of Kansas and it could come out of Minnesota. So, focusing on these companies that are not necessarily going to be domain-specific to world-class institutions like Stanford and Palo Alto or MIT and Cambridge, you can still build real companies in these other interior middle markets. Now, in your role with Thiel Capital as well as somebody who's investing your own capital, you're very close to the family office community and the private capital community. How do you feel family offices right now are reacting to what's happening in the Middle East? What are some themes you've heard? Every family office is different, we know that, but broadly speaking, what's the vibe right now among family offices? Yeah, it's interesting, I was actually there when the war broke out. And so this question hits home probably more closely than it would for most. Where, where were you? I was in the UAE whenever it started. And so it's a very real situation. How did you get out? It was a very complicated, poor man's James Bond escape for me to get out, but I got out. Everything is fine. I have a ton of friends there. It's a place that I've been going to since PayPal days. And so it's surprising to me that markets don't seem to grasp that this is a very real situation. It's a very volatile situation and I hope and I pray that it goes back to some semblance of normalcy soon. But it seems to me that markets are underpricing this volatility and the risk, unfortunately, that it may extend and prolong for a good, good period of time. What makes you think that? Because typically, geopolitical events like the war in Ukraine or the previous war in Iraq, the markets take an initial dip and then we find that over time, it could be six months, 12 months, could be three years, they see through it. And that's what people are saying about this market, coupled with the fact that this market is very tech-dominated, which is unaffected by the Middle East. So why do you think they're underestimating the risk if history tells us these aren't real risks to stock markets? So, markets are usually pretty smart, and I think in this case they still have some catching up to do. Let me give you a specific point. So, I was doing some research on this, and roughly a quarter of AI infrastructure spend from today for the next five years will be coming from the Middle East. Through the sovereign wealth funds and through the capital from all the entities related in the region. A quarter. A quarter. A quarter, so that's the starting point. And then my second point is that roughly half of that spend would be put in the ground, proposed to be put in the ground for data centers in the Middle East itself, and the other half would go to the US and elsewhere. So what would happen, God forbid, if this situation in the Middle East continues, prolongs, and what happens if it gets worse? So, there've already been examples of force majeure of contracts being cancelled throughout that region because of different facilities being hit. So what happens if some of those facilities that get hit start to be data centers? What happens if those—which we've already seen a couple data centers, we've already seen Amazon get hit and you've had a couple others as well. So if some of these data centers start getting postponed or cancelled because of force majeure, what is the impact on the market? And then think about, what is the second-order effect of these financial investments that are undoubtedly tied legally to these CapEx investments? What happens if those get postponed or force majeure cancelled? What is the impact on the market then? Do you think that's bigger than DeepSeek? Do you think that's bigger than the SaaSocalypse? Some of that half of that quarter capital is in the Middle East, the other half everywhere around the world. You think that gush of cash that we've seen from the Middle East could slow? Yeah, the headline would be I think markets have under-appreciated how important the Middle East region is for CapEx spending as it relates to AI and AI infrastructure. And if the Middle East starts taking some of these projects offline or cancelling some of these projects, the impact on the market could be much, much, much larger than what is currently priced in. Where do you think that would first show up? What stocks or what parts of the economy would that show up in? Well, kind of like with private credit, this is not what I do for a living, but just as an amateur I would suggest, you know, Oracle has a very high debt ratio and you have all this kind of circular trading, or what's been alleged as circular trading. So, if you have highly indebted companies that all of a sudden you have projects that are getting postponed or cancelled, their margin for error in terms of their ability to make their payments and keep their credit rating would presumably be relatively slight or thin. And so I think there's a degree of tension and fragility there that if, again, some of these contracts start getting postponed or cancelled, there could be real market impact. And you think the market, whether or not that happens, you don't think the market has priced in that risk? I do not. I think the market is so fixated on all the trees growing in the sky, Goldilocks-type scenarios with AI, which again I'm not a Luddite and I appreciate that optimism as a venture capitalist, but I don't think the risk and the downside is sufficiently priced in. So let's go back to the family offices for a while because again, you know that space. You're a manager in that space, you raise money in that space. Family offices have grown in size and number exponentially over the past 5-10 years. What do you think is the biggest problem that family offices face right now? So I think, in my world, I think family offices and most allocators are very frustrated with venture capital, with venture capitalists. Because we have done as an asset category a poor job of returning our client's money to them in the form of DPI. And so they are understandably very, very frustrated with people like ourselves because they've been waiting on getting their money back for a very long time. And so because of that, a lot of them have said, "Okay, well, I'm going to start doing this job myself." Yes, direct investing is bigger and bigger. And I'm going to become a direct investor. And I think it's unfair when people in my seat criticize them for kind of becoming tourist capital by going direct. Because why shouldn't they? Because they're so frustrated with people like ourselves to have not been returning our capital. So why shouldn't they try themselves, because they couldn't do any worse than a lot of what we've been doing in terms of making investments, not giving money back, having marks on paper, but a lot of their VC fund managers that do it for a living have been doing the same thing. So I think they get a bit of a bad rap in that regard. But I also think there are a lot of VC managers that are frankly not very good. And there needs to be kind of a Darwinian culling of the herd, and that culling has taken in my opinion much, much longer than I would have thought. There's an irony in the sense that VC managers tell their poorly performing companies to fail fast, but they themselves, when they're not performing, fail as slowly as humanly possible. That's true. That's true. And there's a massive disconnect between the two. So I do think there's justifiably a frustration with our family office friends with their venture capitalist investments. What percentage of venture capital firms do you think have no right to be in business? Two-thirds. Two-thirds. Two-thirds? Two-thirds. Well, if you think about it, after the financial crisis, interest rates were zero for 13 plus years and private tech investors, family offices included, associated tech with growth, with yield. So they assumed that if they put more money into venture capital—which by the way is a very, very small asset class. So private equity is a massive asset class, venture capital is a very small subset, and good venture capital, which is the only part you want to invest in, is tiny. But all this money got stuffed into it and guess what? When you stuff a ton of money into an asset class that otherwise cannot sustain it, returns go down. And that's a bad combination and that's where we are, but it's just taken so long for this cathartic process, this culling of the herd, to take place. And you mentioned that because of that performance, family offices have gone to direct investing themselves in a huge way. How have they done? How are family offices at direct, at being direct investors? So, in non-fairness to them, it takes you a very long time to learn that you're not a very good direct private investor. So there's a reason why people do it full-time for a living, and so you're investing... what is crazy to me is that so many of these family offices are investing in these SPV products that are totally opaque, they're extended duration, you have no information rights, and you're basically just taking a punt. And it kind of, it's symbolic in a way of this world that we live in where you have all these betting markets that are out there now. This is essentially in my mind just another version of that—taking a punt—which by the way, there's nothing wrong with that, but it's not the same thing as investing. Investing is about conviction and long-term and not flipping a coin, and these late-stage tech companies through these SPVs in my opinion are much more about trading than they are long-term investing. I mean, what we hear from family offices is that they do direct investing when the principal has an expertise in that field. They may have cashed out of their company 20 years ago, but they still know food packaging or trucking or hotels, whatever it might be, so therefore they're experts. Or number two, they have a good friend who's also in that company and it's a club deal and they trust those people. I'm not sure about that. I think when these fancy people go to their cocktail parties in Manhattan, they have to have something interesting to talk about. And this, and all of their friends are talking about some version of this, so they have to have something to add to the conversation, so therefore they do the same thing. I think. You really, you really think it's sort of... that's part of why I'm not a... this is not a joke. I guarantee you there's a degree to which what this is the dynamic that's going on. Because the Greek shipping magnate that lives in Manhattan knows nothing about rocketry. He knows a lot about ships. So why is he investing in SpaceX? Because his buddy is an expert on rocket ships? Probably not; he just wants to have something fun to talk about at the fancy cocktail party where he meets all of his buddies on Friday night. Whereas if they just say, "Look, I invest in Nvidia stock," anybody can do that—that's not special, that's not cool. That's a good point, because if you have an insatiable AI itch that you have to scratch, why wouldn't you just buy Google? Why would you sign up for a ride in an opaque SPV with fees on fees on fees with no information rights? Versus you literally just click a button and you're into Google, and if you want to get out of Google, you click another button. You can be in and out in milliseconds. So the peer pressure, for lack of a better phrase, I think—I know it sounds like a joke, but I honestly don't think—I think there's an animal spirits component to it where people want to be able to talk as if they're into this shiny new toy, which are these late-stage tech companies. I want to ask you about inflation, because earlier this year when I was talking to a lot of family offices at the conferences, there was a lot more money being put toward inflation bets. In other words, real estate, infrastructure—the idea, I think it was pegged more to tariffs obviously, we have a whole other catalyst now, but the idea was the family offices seemed to believe that inflation was going to stick around longer and remain elevated. And that wasn't the prevailing wisdom on Wall Street, if you look at yields were starting to come down, the Fed was expected to cut, to have a lot of interest rate cuts this year. Do you think that thesis in the family office world is still true? And what, and what is your view of inflation this year and next year? So I think, I think we've all come around to the idea that inflation unfortunately is definitely not transitory. It's here to stay. And I think it absolutely plays into kind of the K-economy dynamic. So, if you're a business that's selling into the top part of the K, you have exceptional pricing power. And conversely, if your business is predicated on inputs that are selling in or related to the bottom of the K, it's very problematic. So in other words, if I had to be very succinct, it's very good to be an owner and operator of the Four Seasons and far, far less so of the Holiday Inn. Because those inflationary pressures, you can only price your hotel room at the Holiday Inn so high, versus with the Four Seasons you have almost inelastic demand to raise your prices for that hotel room. And so you can apply that across all industries, whether you're in the Four Seasons business or the Holiday Inn business. Right, and even beyond the consumer economy, what other areas do you think investors might look toward as a hedge against inflation? Yeah, it's difficult. So if you were long the selling into the top of the K because that demand curve—because I think the wealth gap that already exists, because we're in kind of later innings of capitalism—that wealth gap I think is just getting wider. It's getting more broad. Do you think AI is going to make that more extreme? Well, that's a question I ask literally all the time. It's a rhetorical question, so I think yes. And so, and we live at a time where our friends in DC, you could maybe not too controversially argue, are not doing a great job. Or we in a live in a very divided country, and so the middle class that was such an important component of our society—if they are not being benefited by our friends in DC and AI is making this wealth gap wider, that is very problematic for all of us. And you see that continuing that K-shape, perhaps even accelerated by AI? I think so, and I think the doomers on the AI front—I think that's very overdone. I think again... you don't think there's going to be massive catastrophic job loss. I think again there's a degree to which it's both. And so there's a degree I believe where AI, there's a J-curve and we're going down the J-curve, so it's a bit painful. So we're going through this adjustment curve and then we start going up on the J bit of the curve, then I think it will get quite a bit better. But in this transitional phase, it can feel one way directionally on the downside which is painful, but I think once we start to come back up, I think that will help compensate for it. But in the meantime, it can be difficult. Great. Jack, thanks so much for joining us today. Thank you for having me. Thank you.

Stripe’s Push to Bring AI to Payments, Commerce

Bloomberg Technology(723.0K) ·15:17 ·2.7K views · 2026-04-29
John Collison (President and Co-founder, Stripe) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
Stripe is positioning itself as the primary "economic infrastructure" for the AI era, transitioning from human-centric transactions to a world where AI agents act as independent economic actors through usage-based billing, stablecoin micropayments, and deeper platform integrations.
TL;DR Stripe is positioning itself as the primary "economic infrastructure" for the AI era, transitioning from human-centric transactions to a world where AI agents act as independent economic actors through usage-based billing, stablecoin micropayments, and deeper platform...

TL;DR

Stripe is positioning itself as the primary "economic infrastructure" for the AI era, transitioning from human-centric transactions to a world where AI agents act as independent economic actors through usage-based billing, stablecoin micropayments, and deeper platform integrations.

The Rise of Agentic Commerce

  • AI as an Economic Actor: Collison argues that the economy is shifting from human-to-human transactions to "agentic" commerce, where AI agents make purchasing decisions.
      • Delegated Decisions: Consumers are moving from using AI for research to allowing agents to execute small tasks, such as purchasing domain names or grocery ingredients.
      • B2B Shift: Tech companies (e.g., Cloudflare, Vercel) are increasingly selling services directly to "coding agents" rather than human procurement officers.
  • Google Partnership: Stripe is integrating with Google’s Gemini and AI-powered search to allow users to complete purchases directly within the AI interface rather than being redirected to external websites.
  • Product Discovery: Major brands like Best Buy and Kate Spade are adopting these tools to stay ahead of how AI is changing consumer discovery and checkout habits.

New Economic Models: Tokens and Micropayments

  • Usage-Based Billing: AI has broken the traditional "per-seat" subscription model due to high inference costs.
      • Metered Billing: Companies like OpenAI and Anthropic now require "per-token" or "per-request" billing infrastructure.
      • Metronome Acquisition: Stripe is leveraging its acquisition of Metronome to help AI companies manage these complex, high-volume metering challenges.
  • Stablecoins and "Tempo": While micropayments have historically failed with humans due to "decision friction," they are ideal for AI agents.
      • Streaming Payments: Stripe has incubated a blockchain called Tempo specifically designed for payment use cases.
      • Crypto Utility: Collison views stablecoins as the most viable medium for real-time, autonomous agent transactions.

Security and the "AI Singularity"

  • The Singularity Timeline: Collison semi-seriously dates the "Singularity" to January 1, 2024, noting that AI is now in a state of "recursive self-improvement" where coding models are significantly accelerating the development of the next generation of AI.
  • Evolving Fraud Vectors: AI companies face unique "shrinkage" issues, such as bad actors attempting to steal inference or abuse free trials.
      • Stripe Radar: In a single month, Stripe’s security tools blocked over 3 million attempted thefts targeting top-tier AI clients.
      • Network Effect: Stripe uses its "bird's-eye view" of global internet transactions to identify and block novel AI-specific fraud patterns.

Stripe’s Corporate Strategy and Identity

  • Infrastructure vs. Consumer Brand: Despite having 250 million users on its "Link" checkout service, Stripe remains focused on being the invisible "plumbing" of the internet rather than a consumer-facing app.
  • M&A Stance: Collison declined to comment on reports regarding a potential acquisition of PayPal assets, maintaining Stripe's policy of not commenting on market speculation.
  • Growth Objective: The core mission remains "growing the GDP of the internet" by lowering friction, regardless of whether the buyer is a human or a machine.
"It’s never been the case in technology when you get a way more convenient way of working for consumers that they pick the less convenient one. It’s never happened." — John Collison
"The coding models have gotten so good that they are now contributing in a significant way to the rate of improvement of AI itself... recursive self-improvement is the industry term here." — John Collison

The AI Frontier and How to Spot Billion-Dollar Companies Before Everyone Else — Elad Gil

Tim Ferriss(1.8M) ·101:29 ·7.8K views · 2026-04-29
Elad Gil (CEO, Gil & Co) Tim Ferriss (Host, Tim Ferriss Show)
The AI landscape is currently defined by a "personal IPO" for top talent and a temporary compute ceiling caused by memory bottlenecks, creating a unique two-year window of market parity and a critical 12-18 month exit opportunity for non-durable startups.
TL;DR The AI landscape is currently defined by a "personal IPO" for top talent and a temporary compute ceiling caused by memory bottlenecks, creating a unique two-year window of market parity and a critical 12-18 month exit opportunity for non-durable startups. The Personal...

TL;DR

The AI landscape is currently defined by a "personal IPO" for top talent and a temporary compute ceiling caused by memory bottlenecks, creating a unique two-year window of market parity and a critical 12-18 month exit opportunity for non-durable startups.

The Talent "Personal IPO" and Compute Bottlenecks

  • The Personal IPO: Elad Gil describes a shift where a small class of AI researchers (50–500 people) effectively experienced a personal IPO.
      • Driven by Meta’s aggressive bidding, pay packages have surged to between tens and hundreds of millions of dollars.
      • This wealth allows top researchers to pivot to "big science" or passion projects rather than staying tethered to a single corporate mission.
  • Compute Constraints: All major labs (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google, xAI) are currently hitting a "ceiling" on model scaling.
      • Memory Bottleneck: While chip packaging was the previous constraint, the current bottleneck is High Bandwidth Memory (HBM), largely controlled by Korean manufacturers.
      • Supply Chain Parity: Because no single lab can secure 10x the compute of its rivals due to these supply chain limits, the major players will likely remain at roughly the same capability level for the next two years.
  • The Revenue Sprint: AI companies are reaching scale at unprecedented speeds.
      • OpenAI and Anthropic reached $1 billion in revenue in roughly one year, whereas Google took four.
      • These companies are approaching a run rate representing ~0.1% to 1% of US GDP.

Strategic Timing for Founders and Exits

  • The 12-18 Month Exit Window: Gil advises founders of successful AI companies to look hard at exiting within the next 12–18 months.
      • Historical Precedent: In every cycle (Auto, Dot-com, SaaS), 90-95% of companies eventually go bust.
      • Value Maximization: Many current AI startups may face commoditization or competition from the labs themselves. Selling during this high-valuation window might be the value-maximizing move before the "second derivative of growth" plateaus.
  • Durable Advantage Criteria: Gil identifies what makes a company worth keeping long-term:
      • Workflow Integration: Durability comes from "change management"—embedding the tool so deeply into a company’s workflow that it is hard to extract.
      • Unit of Labor: Generative AI is shifting from selling "seats" (SaaS) to selling "units of labor/cognition," which opens previously closed markets like legal (e.g., Harvey) and health (e.g., Abridge).

The Investor’s Lens: Market over Team

  • Market-First Philosophy: Gil prioritizes market over team (90% of the time). He notes that "amazing teams are crushed by terrible markets, but reasonably crappy teams can do well in great ones."
  • The Power Law: Investment returns are highly concentrated. Gil cites research suggesting that only ~10 companies drive 80% of all technology returns over a 20-year period.
  • Spotting "Open" Markets: AI is currently "consensus" rather than "contrarian." Gil argues that being consensus is currently the smartest move because the technology has "plugged into" all white-collar work and language-based data simultaneously.
  • Aggressive Distribution: Gil debunks the "build it and they will come" myth.
      • Google spent hundreds of millions on browser toolbar distribution.
      • Facebook used aggressive ad strategies in Europe to "buy" network liquidity.
      • Successful companies pair a great product engine with a relentless distribution engine (sales/marketing).

Using AI for Research and Due Diligence

  • AI as a Research Partner: Gil uses multiple models (OpenAI, Claude, Perplexity, Gemini) simultaneously for deep-dive research.
      • Cross-Model Verification: He prompts models to aggregate clinical trial data, provide primary sources, and double-check outputs against one another.
      • "Cold Reading": He experiments with uploading photos of founders to AI to assess personality micro-features (e.g., genuine smiles vs. furrowed brows) to predict behavior and humor, finding surprising signal.
  • Truth-Seeking in Medicine: Gil uses AI to investigate diagnostic shifts, such as the 3,000% increase in autism diagnoses. He found that the primary driver is often diagnostic criteria and school incentives rather than paternal/maternal age.

Notable Quotes

Elad Gil: "There are moments in time where it’s very smart to be contrarian. And there are moments in time where being consensus is the smartest possible thing you can do. And I think right now we’re in a moment in time where being consensus is very right."
Elad Gil: "If your co-founder is kind of like your spouse... your board members are like your in-laws. You have to see them at Thanksgiving... and if they’re an investor, you can’t fire them."
Tim Ferriss: "It just struck me that... if you want money, ask for advice, and if you want advice, ask for money. It goes the other way around, too: if you offer a bunch of advice, oftentimes you get to give money [invest]."
Elad Gil: "We’re going from selling software and SaaS and we’re moving into a world where we’re selling human labor equivalents. We’re selling work hours or labor hours of cognition."
