Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to global stability.
TL;DR Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to...

TL;DR

Jamie Dimon emphasizes that long-term corporate success is built on a "relentless" culture of grit, small autonomous teams, and high-integrity leadership, while warning that geopolitical fragmentation and slipping credit standards currently pose the greatest risks to global stability.

Leadership and Corporate Culture

  • Driving Culture Through Grit: Dimon argues that culture is not about words on a page but about "driving it with grit and courage" in every meeting, hire, and firing.
      • Success stems from a "serve-the-client" scheme rather than a compensation-based scheme.
      • The "Neural Network": JPMorgan functions as a global web where employees can call anyone across the firm for immediate answers, a structure Dimon believes AI cannot replace.
  • Combating Bureaucracy: Bureaucracy, complacency, and arrogance are the primary threats to large organizations.
      • "Rope-a-Dope" Meetings: Dimon cancels meetings where information isn't shared beforehand or where no clear decisions are made.
      • King for a Day: To force action, Dimon asks employees, "If you were king for a day, what would you do?" This prevents people from simply "admiring problems."
      • Eliminating "Jerks": A winning culture requires removing high-performers who are "all about themselves" and ruin team dynamics.
  • Governance and Board Dynamics: To ensure honest feedback, the JPMorgan board meets without Dimon at every meeting.
      • Board presentations are mostly verbal and fast-paced to encourage engagement rather than scripted "preambles."

Risk Management and the Global Economy

  • Concerns Over Private Credit: While not yet a systemic threat at $1.7 trillion, Dimon warns of a "deterioration in underwriting standards" across the industry.
      • He predicts that the next credit recession will be "worse than people think" due to aggressive assumptions and weaker covenants.
  • Geopolitical Fragmentation: Dimon identifies geopolitics—specifically the wars in Ukraine and Iran and the US-China relationship—as the most significant threat to the "free and democratic world."
      • He warns against the fragmentation of Western alliances, noting that "Economic relationship is not just tariffs; it’s investment and regulatory rules."
  • Inflationary Pressures: Dimon remains skeptical that inflation is fully under control, citing global deficits, remilitarization, and green infrastructure needs as persistent inflationary drivers.
      • Stagflation Risk: He refuses to take the "worst-case" scenario of stagflation off the table, suggesting probabilities of higher interest rates are higher than the market expects.

Regional Competitiveness: The European Challenge

  • The "Slow Walk" to Decline: Dimon points out that 25 years ago, the US and European GDPs were equal; today, Europe is only 70% of the US level.
      • The Draghi Report: Dimon urges European leaders to adopt the 300 recommendations in the Draghi Report, specifically the Capital Markets Union and common bankruptcy laws.
  • Scale and Scope: Without a finished common market, European companies will struggle to compete with American and Chinese "juggernauts."
  • Friend-Sourcing: He advocates for "one big, beautiful free trade bill" between the US and Europe to strengthen economic and military alliances.

Technology and the Future of Work

  • AI Integration: JPMorgan has thousands of people deployed on AI use cases ranging from fraud detection to note-taking and hedging.
      • The Cyber Threat: Dimon views cyberattacks as the top technical risk, especially as "bad guys" use AI to find vulnerabilities.
  • Workforce Redeployment: Dimon acknowledges that AI may displace jobs faster than previous technological shifts. He urges governments and companies to focus on "income assistance" and "skills training" rather than just firing workers.
  • The 3.5-Day Work Week: Increased productivity from capital formation and AI will eventually lead to a shorter work week (3.5 days) and higher GDP per person.
      • Return to Office: Despite the long-term trend toward shorter weeks, Dimon remains a firm believer in in-office work for collaboration and socialization.

Notable Quotes

"Bureaucracy, complacency, and arrogance will take down a company. Bureaucracy is like the Petri dish of politics." — Jamie Dimon
"The most significant thing for the future of the free and democratic world is the geopolitics... The fragmentation of that would be the reason a book is written one day, 'How the West Was Lost.'" — Jamie Dimon
"I always ask: 'If you’re king for a day, what are you going to do?' Because people walk in and they admire problems. If you ask them what they’re going to do, for a lot of people, it’s almost crippling to try to answer." — Jamie Dimon
"We're not here to satisfy my employees that they don't want to [come to the office]. We're here for the client." — Jamie Dimon
Well first, I have to say a big thank you to all the speakers today. We have actually had ten of them on the podcast, so you can binge it this weekend. Now, one person we've been trying to get since we started the podcast four years ago is Jamie Dimon. Now, I have a philosophy in life that "no" is a rest stop on the way to "yes," and in this case, it has worked. But just Jamie, before we welcome you onto the stage, just a few snippets here. JPMorgan's origins trace back to 1799, to a company founded to supply water to a growing New York City. It quickly evolved into a bank and shaped its lasting legacy. Over the next two centuries, it grew alongside the economy, financing railroads, supporting industry, and expanding with global trade. But its role became most visible in moments of crisis. In 1907, as panic spread through the financial system, JPMorgan helped organize a response, stabilizing markets at a critical moment and exposing the need for a central bank. Through the Great Depression, it endured one of the most severe economic downturns in modern history. In 2000, the merger of JPMorgan and Chase Manhattan created a new kind of institution. And in 2008, it stepped into the center of the crisis, acquiring Bear Stearns and Washington Mutual. Built on generations of decisions and culture, today JPMorgan Chase stands among the largest and most influential financial institutions in the world. Well, warm welcome. Warm welcome. When we look at these pictures showing the history, the first question has to be, what is the culture that has made you able to be so successful now, after 200 years? First of all, welcome everybody, thrilled to be here. He is persistent, by the way, because when he asked me about could I come in 2025, I said I can't. He said, "What about this date in 2026?" So here I am. But I am thrilled to be here, it's wonderful. So look, cultures are—my whole life I've always been cautious when we talk about culture, because you can say all the words, you can say "employees," "customers," and they don't mean it. So the real way you develop a culture is you're just driving it with grit and courage, like every meeting, everything you do, every trip you make, every person you hire, every person you fire, that it really is about doing the right thing for the customer eventually. Obviously, to do that you've got to take care of your employee. A lot of cultures on Wall Street, as you know, it was about money. How much money can someone make? And some of the things in Wall Street are built on their compensation schemes; they're not a serve-a-client scheme. And so I've just been relentless. When we first did the JPMorgan Bank One merger in 2004, I had a book of how we do business and Bill Harrison, who was the chairman at the time, said, "You gotta send it out." And I was like, "No, let's do it first. Let's let people experience the detail, the diligence, the fortress, the balance sheet, all these things, the follow-up, the open meetings, that you have to speak up." It's not even a choice, hopefully. And if you do that, you just drive a certain type of culture. You say you have to fight it every day. Yeah. What are the kind of things you do in meetings? So you've got to get out, so you do it in the meeting or on a bus trip or on a road trip, that you're talking to your people. Everyone knows that you're only trying to do the right thing for the company and the client. So no one at the company can say something that will insult me, as long as they say it because they think it's in the best interest of the company or the client. That, I didn't bring my list, but that we used to take a trip, I'd come back with a long list of stuff. As a matter of fact, my management committee doesn't like it when I come back from a trip because they know I'm about to lay on them. Why do we do this? And why do we do that? And why do we do this? And this is across technology and just about everything else you do. But even in that room, you know, I tell people at my management team, the agenda is your agenda. Don't say to me you didn't put it on the agenda. You have to say that everything important is on this list. And it actually puts the burden on the other people to say, "Well you skipped this," or "What about that issue?" or something. So every business has a follow-up list, and every business when it travels around the world knows each other. And it's hard work, you know? I mean it's harder than I thought, but looking back, you actually do build a common culture. Why is it so hard? Because you have to be relentless. I mean, if you, you know, when I do go around, I mean I try not to let even one little thing skip my attention. So if there's a, you know, if I read a customer complaint and someone says to me, "Why are you paying attention to that? It's so minor," I say, "Well I'm paying attention to that because we might be doing the exact same thing to a million people." It's not that minor. You know, or it was a teller who told us something that shortened how long it takes to open an account. I've been on buses with management teams and they don't want to hear from the teller or the branch manager, you know, what's going on in the branch because they find it insulting, whereas I look at them and I realize they probably shouldn't have that job. What's the thing you still have on your sheet which you haven't managed to solve? I have a list of calls I need to make, a list of people who owe me something. That's the most important one. And that one is relentless. Well I for sure owe you one now, after this. No no, not owe me like that, like I've asked a question and I'm waiting for a follow-up. I want to know why this happened, or why that happened, and did we fix this customer complaint. It could be on anything. Then I have a list of things I need to think about. I have a list of things which are going to go on another list but I just jotted it down because, so it's follow-up dates, calls, immediate issues, and I probably burn through that list once every two or three days. Yeah. And then there's a much longer one, which you, by business, I carry a little folder which has one by each business, much more detailed. Like very detailed. You say in your—by the way, you write a fantastic letter every year. And in the letter which recently came out, you talk about bureaucracy and the way you have to, you have to fight it. How do you stop bureaucracy from creeping into the organization? Because you are now, it's a huge organization, right? Yeah. I really believe this: bureaucracy, complacency and arrogance will take down a company. Bureaucracy is like the Petri dish of politics and everything else. And there are, and by the way, you could be a small company and have it. You could be a big company and have it. You can have it in your branch, you can not have it in the other branch. And it's always the manager's fault. I mean, almost always. The way you fight it is with me, all the information is shared beforehand. So you know, there's no secret. I remember going to companies and, you know, this wouldn't share with that part of the company. If it isn't shared properly, I can't—I generally just cancel the meeting. That, you know, if someone comes in and says, you know, "Nicolai wants X and I don't believe that that's right," I say, "Well why did he wait for this meeting to do that? Go talk to him." Everything, no matter how small, get on the road, go, go see clients. Clients are a gift because, you know, they're, they're demanding, they should be, but they also tell you, in our case, what our competitors are doing better. Why we didn't get something. What, you know, if we don't do this in that country, you're not going to give us a big piece of business because of how important that is for you. And I uniquely know, it might cost 30 million dollars to build a payment system to hook up to a country. But it's easy to say, "Well we're going to do it now." I just found out why the staff isn't doing it. And so when you have a meeting, people often don't know who's running it. That's a mistake. When you have a meeting and someone ends the meeting by saying, "That was a great meeting, we'll pick it up again next week," it's usually a bad meeting. The meeting should end with, "Okay David, you're going to do X, talk to these people," not hierarchical. It's just, you know, cut across the company, you talk to HR, you know, consumers got the most people, this change in the program is going to affect, you know, their branches, talk about it, come back, make a recommendation. I always ask if you're king for a day, what are you going to do? Because people walk in and they admire problems. If you ask them what they're going to do they say, "Well I looked at this, I look at that," I say, "No no, what would you do?" And honestly, for a lot of people it's just almost crippling to, to try to answer that question. And so have the right people in the room. Very often people are making decisions, like people think you go back to your office and you think. No, usually you iterate over and over and over and it gets you to the right place. And if you don't have the right people in the room, it will not happen. Talking about the right people in the room, you say teams should be small, like Navy SEALs, right? Right. Very little politics, meaning also we don't do a lot of super presentations where we just run through how great you are. Like I think you should celebrate all the time, but when you're in a management meeting with me, it's what does the other guy do better. Stripe kicked our butt, PayPal kicked our butt. Okay, I can go one after another. So no matter how good we think we are, I point out who's doing better. And you know, we're the number one FX trader in the world, but we're number seven in Vietnam. Why is that? And so you really dig, drill down, and you find out what you're not doing well, what people aren't collaborating on. And then you have to get rid of the jerks. You know, and if you don't, no one believes you. And that takes a long time. Who are the jerks? What makes a jerk? It's all about them. They admire problems. I say there are good bureaucrats, too. They're great at process, but they like the process, not the outcome. Whereas I like the outcome. I like, or like, and also I look at the inputs. You know, when you talk about business you talk about your output. Your output is your performance, your branch, things like that, but what was the input? Did you train the person properly? Did we build the right technology? Why didn't you put a branch, why didn't we put more bankers in Oslo, why didn't we put more? The input will drive the business for twenty years. The output is just the result of what you did all that time. And so but the sharing part eliminates a lot of it. Not allowing rope-a-dope meetings, and, like I said, they're good bureaucrats but some people it's always about them. They simply can't get beyond the fact that they want—they're always talking about like the next job and the next thing and pounding their chest. Obviously men do that more than women. But you see these diseased behaviors. And so and I have to earn my trust and respect every day, too. It isn't like I walk in a room and somehow you have to trust me. You don't. You know, you're going to be watching closely what does the boss do, what does he say, does he really mean it, does he follow up? And hopefully I'm trusted by people and they know it's okay to say anything to me. Doesn't bother me whatsoever. I say that to regulators, prime ministers, bankers, tech people, I'm just trying to do a better job, that's it. My board, and this is important for governance, my board, I've been doing this since I got to Bank One in 2000, meets without me every single meeting. I asked them to do it because, you know, I'm a little forceful, I have my opinions. But every time there's a big decision, I have the other senior executives come in and say, "Tell them what you think." Tell them, I want you, and they say what they think. And so they might disagree with me on a people decision or some risk decision or something like that. After that, I leave the room and the board meets, 100% without me, and usually the lead director usually gives me a call afterwards or David Novak, when he was lead director, he would give me a handwritten note, you know, like he'd say a little coaching, what I could do different, what they want to hear about, what they're concerned about. And it made me do a better job. I was never worried about it. They're allowed to talk to any and all senior management. And no one, I have never done a presentation at the board, at least not since JPMorgan that I can remember. And no, so they do it all. And I let them do it. I make comments afterwards but I don't—I don't do preambles. I don't say that, you know, "This person's going to talk about A, B, C, D, and E," and then say, "Why don't you talk about A, B, C, D, and E." I say, "Go ahead, shoot." You know, and keep it fast. A lot of the presentations are verbal. So if you went in there you'd hear the person say, "Hey, in investment banking we did great, fixed income, let me talk about how we're going to expand bankers in these eight cities with these various things. We think over time they'll deploy so much capital and so much thing. Here's the risk associated with it. We looked at other options, A and B, but we'd pick this one, C." So they just are verbal. And the board loves it, by the way. I asked the CEO of one of the other big American banks what made JPMorgan really special, and he said, "Well it's about, it's about the firm, it's not about the individual people." How do you install that? I think that, you know what it is, and it's I look at all—we all know sports teams, and when you see a sports team, you know, jiving, or you guys look at football, or American football or your football or basketball, any sports team, you know when the team's working. They're doing their job, they learn their plays, they all want to win, they pass off, and you know when there's someone on that team who's just ruining it for everybody. And business is the same thing, except you can bullshit about it. Literally. "We did a great job, we're the best person, the reason we didn't do as well as Goldman did is because of A, B, and C." I always say yeah, it's because they're better, generally. And but you know it when you see it, and you know that person who is ruining it. That's the team. And you know, I just wear the jersey. I'm like the—I'm not even the senior coach, I'm like the general manager. You know, that we have people running the businesses who are actually like they're the quarterbacks. They're making the decisions and but if you look at the team, they communicate all the time. I wrote in my letter about JPMorgan's like a neural network. So Liz over here, she can call anyone around—she knows people all around the world to help our clients and they'll get an immediate answer. And you know, it could be different balance sheet, different currency, different problem, local revolver, a great idea for an M&A that someone wasn't thinking about, and that neural network—you can't replace it, by the way. AI won't be able to replace it, at least not for a long time. You say the teams need to operate a bit like Navy SEALs. Yeah. What do you mean by that? So I think you have to have the best of both. You've got to have small teams that are fully authorized, dedicated to accomplish something. So I remember trying to do digital account opening years ago, and the first reaction you get is tech says okay, AI of this person says okay, sales says okay, marketing says okay, branch system says okay, it's 1% of each person's job. It will not get it done. You need dedicated people authorized to build an account, a digital opening. And that is the team. They obviously have to rely on lots of people, but they have what they need on the team. That might be a lawyer in some place, it might be compliance, it might be something like that. It's hard to do. You also need the platform. You know, you can't, we can't all have our own separate editions of applications and stuff like that, so you kind of need the best of both. Kind of like these Navy SEAL teams, they all have common equipment, common languages, common intelligence, common—they don't pick their own guns and their own tanks. That's common, you know, and so you've got to have the best of both worlds. But you've got to make sure that the common one doesn't create an enormous bureaucracy that kills the ability of the team. That does happen. Everyone wants to review it, everyone wants to go through it, and so you have to have like little war rooms. Okay, we're going to pick: what are we going to do this with Databricks or Snowflake, are we going to do this? Get the people in the room and work it out. WATCH LIVE: Senate Banking Committee votes on Kevin Warsh's nomination for Federal Reserve chair

PBS NewsHour(5.3M) ·20:56 ·3.7K views · 2026-04-29
Kevin Warsh (Nominee for Federal Reserve Chair)
The Senate Banking Committee voted 13-11 to advance Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair to the full Senate, following a contentious debate regarding central bank independence and allegations of executive interference by the Trump administration.
TL;DR The Senate Banking Committee voted 13-11 to advance Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair to the full Senate, following a contentious debate regarding central bank independence and allegations of executive interference by the Trump administration. The Vote:...

TL;DR

The Senate Banking Committee voted 13-11 to advance Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair to the full Senate, following a contentious debate regarding central bank independence and allegations of executive interference by the Trump administration.

Committee Vote and Procedural Context

  • The Vote: The committee moved in-block to report Kevin Warsh favorably for a seat on the Board of Governors (term expiring 2040) and a four-year term as Chairman.
      • The final tally was 13 in favor and 11 opposed, reflecting a strict party-line split.
  • The Trigger for the Markup: Senator Elizabeth Warren (Ranking Member) noted that the vote only proceeded after US Attorney Jenny Durkan announced the closure of a criminal investigation into current Fed Chair Jerome Powell, a move Warren characterized as a political "trigger" to allow the nomination to move forward.
  • DOJ Assurances: Senator Thom Tillis defended the timing, stating he received personal assurances from the Department of Justice that the investigation into the Fed was being handled appropriately by the Inspector General, effectively resolving his previous concerns about the probe.

Allegations of Executive Overreach

  • Independence Concerns: Democrats, led by Senators Warren and Raphael Warnock, argued that advancing Warsh facilitates an "illegal attempt to seize control" of the Federal Reserve.
      • Threats to Personnel: Critics highlighted President Trump’s repeated threats to fire Chair Powell and his ongoing legal attempts to remove Governor Lisa Cook.
      • Ongoing Investigations: Senator Warren entered statements into the record from the President and DOJ officials suggesting that criminal investigations into current Fed leadership are "not dropped" but merely paused or shifted to different agencies.
  • The "Sock Puppet" Critique: Senator Warren labeled Warsh a "Trump sock puppet," citing his refusal during hearings to acknowledge that Donald Trump lost the 2020 election as evidence that he lacks the independence required for the role.

Candidate Fitness and Financial Transparency

  • Financial Disclosures: Senator Warren raised concerns over Warsh’s personal finances, noting he holds more than $100 million in "secret financial assets."
      • Divestment Plan: Critics argued Warsh has failed to disclose if his plan to divest involves significant financial backing from billionaire investors.
  • Past Performance: Opponents pointed to Warsh's tenure as a Fed Governor during the 2008 financial crisis, describing his record as "catastrophic" for American families while favoring Wall Street.
  • Refusal to Answer "Hypotheticals": Senator Warnock expressed deep dissatisfaction with Warsh’s written responses to questions.
      • Firing Scenario: When asked if he would leave the Fed if the President attempted to unlawfully fire him, Warsh responded that he would "not answer hypothetical questions," a stance Warnock called "woefully insufficient" given the President's actual attempts to fire sitting governors.

Economic Outlook and "Bidenomics"

  • Republican Perspective: Republican members argued that Warsh is "battle-tested" and uniquely qualified to lead the country out of the "bind of Bidenomics."
      • Inflation Control: Proponents blamed the previous administration for 9% inflation and argued that Warsh would provide a more consistent, less political focus on the needs of American households.
  • Democratic Perspective: Senator Warnock countered that the current "Trump economy" is failing working families, particularly in Georgia, and that the Fed must remain independent to protect jobs and affordability rather than serve the President’s "poll numbers."

Mr. Chairman, we should not be voting today on Kevin Warsh's nomination to lead the Federal Reserve. The Trump economy is in real trouble. Inflation is up, job creation is down, the stink of stagflation is in the air, and President Trump is getting desperate. A vote today by this committee to advance Mr. Warsh will bring the President one step closer to completing his illegal attempt to seize control of the Fed and to artificially juice the economy. Trump has not been subtle about his takeover. He's threatened to fire the Fed chair, he's actually tried to fire a Fed governor, and he started not one but two bogus criminal investigations to push them out. When this committee refused to move on his new nominee under such circumstances, US Attorney Jenny Durkan announced that she would, quote, "close the bogus criminal investigation into Fed chair Powell while the Federal Reserve inspector takes up the inquiry." This was the trigger for getting to today's markup. Now, I understand wanting to believe that there is finally an end to the President's demands to take over the Fed. But no one is fooled. Trump is still going after control of the Fed and he is keeping the threat of bogus criminal charges alive until he gets what he wants. This is particularly important when it comes to maximizing employment and making sure folks can afford to provide for their families. The written answers I and others on this committee got back were so woefully insufficient that I was left wondering if Mr. Warsh even read our questions. Did he take this process seriously? I want to read just one example. I asked, quote, "if President Trump or any future president attempts to unlawfully fire you without cause, would you leave? Would you leave the Federal Reserve?" His response, quote, "I will not answer hypothetical questions of this nature." That was his answer. Well, this isn't a hypothetical question. In fact, the President attempted to fire Governor Cook in the past year, and the President has repeatedly mused about firing Chair Powell because he won't bend to his interest rate demands, doing so as recently as two weeks ago. And Mr. Warsh says he won't answer hypothetical questions. These are the sorts of answers that matter when looking at the future of the Fed and whether the body remains an independent steward of the American economy. This matters to millions of families all across this country, even as they're trying to make their lives work in this tough Trump economy. America's markets are the envy of the world. Investors flock to the United States in large part because they trust that the Federal Reserve board is free from political influence and political pressure. And working families in Georgia and across the country struggling in this Trump economy deserve to know that the Fed is on their side, on their side, maximizing their chances to keep a good paying job and keeping their lives affordable, not on the side of the President's poll numbers or his political concerns as we approach the midterm. Fed independence is not theoretical. It matters to the everyday lives of working families. And for that reason, I vote no. Thank you, Mr. Chair. Yes, sir, thank you. The ayes have it.
